While things may seem \\"too out there\\" or intimidating at first, just keep sharpening your techniques and practice the easier, but still ridiculously, delicious recipes in the DOMESTIC portion of the book until you can garner enough courage to try something uncomfortable/fun.<br /><br />Hope this review helps.<br /><br />This is a great book, and at over 650 pages is also a monster of a book.<br /><br />Cheers.

0739432176|5.0|In a nutshell (the story at least): 18-year-old Sabriel is forced to rise up to save the day when her father (a clever necromancer) goes missing. She'll have to test those skills that were learned from he himself, deal with powerful enemies, ride along with a hazy character, who may or may not be on her side, and meet the man of her dreams in the process. The loving are the daring after all, aren't they? It is another coming of age story, might be said. Well, this one is far superior than most.<br /><br />Daughter of a wise Necromancer, Sabriel one day receives her father's tools, bells and a sword, and must go to his aid. She goes into the Ancient Kingdom, a land of magic, dead, good, and evil. She is joined by a cat, and meets a prince who'd been cursed (kiss a frog? Or in this case, a figurehead). Together, they fight their way onward and through many hardships, and that unexpected ultimate loss, they vanquish evil, and restore peace in the Ancient Kingdom.<br /><br />Another little girl in another famous story (Alice in Wonderland) found herself in a strange land, dealing with strange situations and creatures. Here, although Sabriel does not have all the advantages of the age she lived in (on her side of the wall), and although she finds thrown into a stranger, darker and much more dangerous place, she is never a victim or an ing√©nue damsel in distress: she knows that she relies on those fatherly teachings and knows that this is her quest and her ordeal, facing any doubt whether she is really prepared to be and do what is expected of her, she uses her own resolve and wit, a little magic, and that enigma of a cat.<br /><br />The story shows (maybe a better verb is Sings) how life is not black and white, how people can't always choose sides, how no matter what's going on around you, you must look into yourself and find your own strength. It deals with loss, grief, moving on, with adolescence and with how finding that first love means more the discovery of ourselves in others, and sharing with us the delight of that recognition.<br /><br />Usually, male characters are painted as an extension of what women think the perfect man should be, but, hey, being realistic, who would really want to spend her life with a vampire who can't help but crooning the world, and whispering to you, how much he loves you? Wouldn't you rather journey with a man of honor, of courage, who shows his love rather than talk about it? Instead of being changed or improved by him and the relationship, who wouldn't rather be that strong young woman who finds her might, who can fight against adversity without super powers? Talk about a role model girls should vie for.<br /><br />I do believe that from all those stories that form the tapestry of our experiences, the fantasy ones (such as this one) exemplify realistically human nature, how every decision has consequences, the importance of honor, honor as in ancient times, honor gained through your actions, your words, honor among your peers. Fantasy is a necessary ingredient in life because it reflects everything we are or want to be. In Sabriel, the world is defenseless against all they have forgotten, except for the people close to the wall, who still remember, no one stands a chance. And even they are in grave danger.<br /><br />They say that the test of a book is dreading to begin the last chapter, and I found myself clutching those very last pages wishing that this one had no end, that that dreaded last chapter lingered. So much that I named my own cat Mogget to honor that paradigm of smartness and bravery.

If you are looking for top quality slide scans, this unit is not for you.<br />I may keep it to use for 35mm negatives to scan.

1451620829|4.0|Okay, I am a pretty huge Kiss fan. They were the first band I got into as a young kid of 11 years-old. I discovered the band in 1977 right around the time Kiss Alive II was released, and I heard it at a friend's house. I promptly went out and purchased their latest studio album Love Gun, and became an instant fan. Prior to that, it was music like the Grease soundtrack, and the Bee Gees, and other poppy disco stuff of the time. So, Kiss was my first taste of hard rock, and I consumed all they had.<br /><br />They were my first concert experience, seeing the Dynasty tour in 1979 and many times after that. The only thing I really knew about the band was what I saw. Not an avid reader, nor a real follower of the latest tabloid/magazine world, I did not spend much time following the behind the scenes publicized dirt. They were super heroes to me - amazing musicians who put on a great show.<br /><br />Now, 35 years later, I am still a big fan. I have all of their albums, I have a bunch of merchandise too. I follow them religiously to a degree. Obviously I like hundreds of other bands now, but still Kiss ranks up their as a key player in my eye. I have followed the solo stuff, I have followed the former members (Ace & Peter), but still, only paid a small amount of attention to the publicized dirt.<br /><br />I bought Gene's book, [[ASIN:0609810022 Kiss and Make-Up]], but never read it. I did read his other book, [[ASIN:1597775029 Sex Money Kiss]], which I laughed off as just a bunch of his egotistical self-centered money-hungry crap that it was. I read Ace's biography [[ASIN:1451613954 No Regrets]] a few months back, and it really focused a lot more on his own personal struggle. The early formative years of Kiss were brought to life as seen through his eyes, but overall, the book seemed more centered around his own personal dark struggles, with not quite as much bad-mouthing as we find in this book by Peter.<br /><br />After finishing Peter's book, I have to say, my opinion on the band is drastically twisted. I am torn between two opinions. First, on one side, Peter was self-destructing and a real arrogant jerk who seemed to care less about others or even himself. With the amount of drugs and trash that he got into, just like I felt with Ace's book, it is surprising these two are still alive. Peter made so many bad decisions, he acted so ignorantly, impulsively, and abusively that it makes me despise him for the most part, and I often feel like he got what he deserved. Before, during and after Kiss, he just always seemed like a jerk.<br /><br />On the other hand though, after hearing the horrible stuff that Gene and Paul put him through, I can almost feel sorry for him, and that stuff makes me lose all respect for and despise them. But still, I see two sides fighting head-to-head, with Peter being a jerk, and Paul/Gene also being major jerks continually. It is hard to tell if this was cause and effect or not. As a musician myself, I can relate to some of the behind the scenes aspects of it. If I were like Gene, a sober, serious musicians looking at the business aspect of all that gets done (which I do tend to be), would I react the same way if a member of my band was a drugged out jerk-wad always getting in trouble and soiling the band's name and image? Probably so.<br /><br />Were Gene and Paul reacting to the horrible lifestyles of Peter and Ace, and that pressure caused more tension backlash from Peter and Ace? That seems to be the story we hear from one side. But Peter's story at times makes you feel Gene and Paul were abusive from the start, regardless of the drugs, etc. My real question is, with the amount of drugs and alcohol that Peter and Ace claim to have been constantly consuming all those years, how could they really remember very much about all of these details as they claim to in their books? Maybe their perspective is greatly clouded by their activity, and so they think things were worse than they were. It is tough toreally say, since it becomes just his word against his word against his word. Now Paul is working on his biography, so we can soon add a fourth version. Paul and Gene were sober, so could their telling be more accurate? Or are they just twisting it to make their selves look good. Were they taking advantage of the always drugged up guys all along?<br /><br />Peter seemed fairly clean and sober during the reunion tour, so for sure it seems they really screwed him and Ace during that time period, making Gene and Paul out as simply appalling people, which seems to be not just coming from Peter's words only. The trail of disgruntled former Kiss members can attest to there being some kind of real issue with the Gene/Paul team. I will chime in on the current replacement members wearing the Cat and Spaceman makeup - it is despicable and reprehensible. I have seen the band live twice with the new guys (I know, somewhat hypocritical to do that), and the band as a unit is amazing, and the new music they make is great. But there would have been no sense of fun lost at ALL if Tommy and Eric had their own characters, just like Eric Carr and Vinnie had. I believe it would have been a much more professional and honorable thing for them to have done, rather than allowing new people to wear the characters of the original members. After reading this book, I see this action as purposely being a personal attack against Peter and Ace - again, showing Gene and Paul to be truly horrible people.<br /><br />I have followed all of the seasons of Gene's Family Jewels show, and have gotten to somewhat know the man more than I have from just years of listening to his music. I found him to be quite human and loveable, and at times felt sorry for him on the show. Occasionally you get a peak at the hard-edged business man on the show, but these books show more of that side than the show ever does. The show gives us some of the silliness and family side of things, but at times I feel it might be scripted and set to be more entertainment than true reality. Peter's book takes away all of that love-ability that I built up from his show, and flushes it down the toilet. Gene comes out as a truly scumbag, bottom-feeding, womanizing, self-gratifying despicable character.<br /><br />In the end, Peter is/was a self-destructive, horrible person who made a lot of bad mistakes, abused himself and others frequently, and did a lot of wrong to a lot of people for a lot of years. It sounds like now he is on the road to settling down from that lifestyle, and the last section of the book discussing his relationship with God is a promising step forward, but the damage done in his wake is a long way from being rectified, and he still comes out looking like a bad guy through this all. So, he has opened the door to really reveal the filth that was his own life, and in the process whines incessantly about how he was treated.<br /><br />The fantasy is gone - the band Kiss will now forever be tainted in my mind. No longer are they just this cool, mysterious band of larger-than-life excitement on stage, but they are now a bunch of low-life self-pleasing jerks who only look out for their own best interests regardless of who they have to plow over to get what they want. Maybe this is nothing new for a rock band, but it really destroys the view of my first-loved rock band. Will I still listen to them? Yes, absolutely - the fantasy lives on in the music, tainted as my mind may be now.<br /><br />Oh, and one last comment. After reading this book, Peter last album, 2007's [[ASIN:B000RIHBI4 One for All]], which I admit I frowned on from the start and did not consider it to be much at all, takes on an entirely new life to me, and now I truly enjoy it much more, understanding the background that led to it. Give it a spin if you haven't in a while.really say, since it becomes just his word against his word against his word. Now Paul is working on his biography, so we can soon add a fourth version. 0307352145|5.0|Do you hear it?  One third of the population is standing, applauding this monumental work by Susan Cain.  You probably can't hear it.  The applause is subdued, but heart-felt.  Most of the applauders are standing alone in their living room, a fire crackling in the fireplace, and the television is off.  They like it that way - the alone part, the fireplace part, and non-television part.  They are the introverts of the world, and they just finished reading the first book dedicated to explaining their sensitive, reflective selves.  The book is entitled, Quiet: The Power of Introverts In a World That Can't Stop Talking.<br /><br />At 268 pages of text, 271 End Notes, and a full index, the book covers a lot of material, and much of it in depth.  The book is no dry textbook, though.  Susan Cain shares much of her own story, including her discovery of her strengths and weaknesses as an introverted attorney. She shares her journey to understand those similar to her, the introverts in the world.  Throughout much of the book she shares her firsthand accounts of meeting the researchers who have spent their careers understanding introversion and extroversion.<br /><br />I was hooked in the introduction.  A quick self-assesment concluded that I, indeed, fit the bill for introversion.  I couldn't agree more with statements like,\\"I dislike small talk, but I enjoy talking in depth about topics that matter to me, \\" and \\"I prefer not to show or discuss my work with others until it's finished.\\"<br /><br />In the first part of the book, Cain attempts to illustrate what she calls the \\"American Extrovert Ideal,\\" by highlighting the work of Dale Carnegie (\\"How to Win Friends and Influence People\\"), business models that force employees to work in teams, and business schools that coerce the \\"quiet kids\\" into constant collaboration.  Cain even visited an evangelical mega-church and concluded that much of American Christianity doesn't work for introverts.  She almost lost me in this first section.  The tone was humorous, but still a bit reactionary.  I realize that she was building her case, but in doing so, her conclusions were much too broad.  For instance, in the area where I have the most experience, the Church, I think she got it wrong!  In fact, in reflection on her mega-church visit, she quotes her host - a fellow introvert and pastor (who does not attend the church they visited) who says, \\"There was no emphasis on quiet, liturgy, ritual, things that give you space for contemplation.\\"  Hence, their discomfort in a public worship service.  Were they to dig deeper, they would find that this church teaches those contemplative practices to their members as they grow in their faith.  And while I am a raging introvert, I have to say that I do not prefer to worship publicly in a quiet setting featuring formal liturgy.  To suggest that introverts prefer a certain style of worship is to push general conclusions too far.  And if this was the case in an area where I have a bit of knowledge, I wonder where else this was the case.  However, she makes a strong case for the struggle of most introverts to find their place in American culture.<br /><br />In part two, Cain shares some of the relevant research on introversion.  I especially connected with the research of Jerome Kagan.  Kagan discovered that introverts tend to be very sensitive to external stimuli- almost over-stimulated - in that we observe everything around us, and then, having reached stimuli-capacity quicker than the extroverts around us, we need to retreat to a non-stimulating place in order to reflect, rest, and re-charge our ability to be stimulated.  Kagan noted especially that introverts are very alert, very sensitive, and experience complex emotions. That is so me!<br /><br />Cain's book is not just stories and theory.  In part four, she suggests strategies and practices that introverts can adopt to thrive and succeed in a world that is often uncomfortable for them.  She shares the secretto public speaking (and no, it has nothing to do with imagining the audience's clothing, or lack thereof).  She shares networking strategies for introverts who really want to \\"work a room.\\"  Finally, she shares strategies for parenting an introvert.  (I think I have one - I better re-read this chapter)  I would suggest that the reader only read the chapters in this section that directly interest them.  Honestly, the book feels a little too long at this point.<br /><br />I heartily recommend this to any introvert who wants to better understand themselves and learn to succeed in a world filled with extroverted success.  I would also recommend it to the spouse of an introvert.  This work will remain a standard for years!<br /><br />Required FTC Disclaimer: I received this book for free from WaterBrook Multnomah Publishing Group for this review.  That means they gave me a free copy of the book in exchange for my honest opinion about it.

0143113100|5.0|The book, \\"The Brain that Changes Itself\\" is written by Norman Doidge, M.D. Doidge is a psychiatrist, psychoanalyst, and researcher at Columbia University for Psychoanalytic Training and Research in New York and the University of Toronto's department of psychiatry. He is also an author, essayist, and a poet. \\"The Brain that Changes Itself\\" deals primarily with brain plasticity. Neuroplasticity is the ability for the brain to change through learning and experience. Doidge writes that our brain is able to learn new things, overcome disabilities, and recover despite the long held belief that our brain was fixed. The book does a great job explaining topics in a simple way. Instead of the book being heavily loaded with scientific terms, Doidge was able to break down the science so readers from all types of backgrounds will be able to enjoy and understand.<br />For many years, neuroscientists believed that functions of the brain were localized.  In other words, each part of the brain has its own role and function.  This theory is not entirely wrong because scientists discovered that there are certain areas that are responsible for certain functions. However, new studies have shown that the brain is plastic. If a certain pathway in the brain is damaged, the brain has the ability to find alternative pathways and adapt to the change. Doidge includes personal stories of several patients who prove that plasticity exists. One of the patients name is Cheryl Schiltz who's vestibular apparatus, a sensory organ for the balance system, was not working. To treat her condition, she received an artificial receptor that developed new paths and learned to recognize objects, perspective, and movement. As a result, it can be seen that the sensory cortex is plastic and adaptable to change. Another patient, Barbara Arrowsmith Young had exceptional abilities that co-existed with retardation. She began to exercise her weak areas simply by practicing certain exercises to strengthen her disabilities. She practiced cognitive assessments to sharpen her brain and was able to overcome her disabilities.  Through case studies, Doidge shows how the brain is malleable, thus it is plastic.<br />Much of the content that is discussed in the book has been covered in our class. Doidge mentions the action potential, and states that the neurons that fire together are the ones that wire together and are therefore stronger. The \\"use it or lose it\\" principle is mentioned when discussing cognitive assessments, as done in Barbara's case study. If we learn a new skill but never practice it, we lose it. Most common example of this phenomenon deals with language. If we acquire the skill of learning a new language but decrease the usage of if later on in life, we will forget to speak it. Doidge also discusses the communication between the brain and the nervous system, sensory and motor function, and functions of frontal lobes. Much of this is discussed in Cheryl Schiltzs' condition since sensory and motor receptors played a large part in her disability. The book introduces new material, but Doidge includes well-written and explained background information that was discussed in class beforehand.<br />If readers are looking for an interesting, yet light read that deals with neuroscience, I would highly recommend \\"The Brain that Changes Itself.\\" I especially liked how almost each chapter had a personal story of patients that dealt with disorders, but were able to overcome them through neuroplasticity. Barabara Arrowsmith Young was one of the personal stories who was labeled to be \\"retarted\\" before she learned how to get better. She suffered from many problems; her Broca's area was not functioning properly, she lacked spatial reasoning and had a kinesthetic problem, she had a visual disability, and most of all, she had trouble understanding grammar, math concepts, logic, and cause and effect. However, she forced herself to work through her disabilities. She developed a system in which she underwent hours of assessments that helped brain functions that are weak. Essentially, she was able to change her brain through learning. I think that by including personal, success stories, I was more intrigued in reading the book. If someone is looking for specific research on brain plasticity, this book is highly recommended. Not only does the book talk about neuroplasticity, but major discoveries of neuroscience are also included. The long held belief that we are born with neurons that will never grow is now old information. The book discussed points that are highly discussed in the area of neuroscience without making the reader feel like they are reading a scientific research paper.<br />In conclusion, I enjoyed the book very much.  It was fascinating to read how neuroplasticity was rejected so many times by other scientists and now we read about it in our textbooks. Thanks to that breakthrough, thousands of lives were changed. Through neuroplasticity, patients are able to work with the areas that are weak and have them progress. The idea of that itself is fascinating enough because it makes me wonder what kinds of technology and new approaches we will have in the future. \\"The Brain that Changes Itself\\"  is a read that provides an important discovery and the truly fascinating function and adaptability of the human brain.

B00917IQFY|4.0|For three albums - Heat Treatment (1976), Stick to Me (1977) and Squeezing Out Sparks (1979) - Graham Parker and The Rumour were one of the very best bands in the world. Great songs delivered with passion and huge intensity. Sure, they lacked subtlety - everybody played all through all the songs - but it was so full-on, so exciting that it didn't matter.<br /><br />What happened? Lack of the commercial success they richly deserved led to frustration and to problems with their record company. Other acts in approximately the same genre (notably Elvis Costello and Joe Jackson) succeeded to a much greater extent. Just when GP&TR should have been stepping up another level to compete, they stepped down with a mediocre record, The Up Escalator (1980). They soon broke up, and GP embarked on a moderately successful solo career, but he never recaptured the early magic.<br /><br />So a new album from GP&TR, their first in 32 years, is a hugely enticing prospect, but also a rather risky one for a fan devoted to those three classic albums. My expectations from GP&TR are so high that there is not much chance they can be met.<br /><br />And so it proves. The album is nothing like their great 1970s trio. It's produced by much older men - slower, quieter more reflective. It reminds me of much of GP's solo work, in fact. So it's pretty good to very good (as is almost all of GP's solo work), but there are no sparks being squeezed out, and they are not pouring it all out. It's a good album, but if you were hoping to rekindle that adrenaline-rush feeling that GP&TR used to generate in the late 1970s, you'll be disappointed. If you like intelligent, witty songs performed by great musicians, you'll enjoy this.

By the time the resolution comes around, it makes for a very satisfying and surprisingly touching finish. Don't expect a Best Picture winner, but MiB3 has enough working for it to offer nearly everyone a highly enjoyable experience.

1469984202|5.0|NO SPOILERS!<br /><br />Let's say this at the start: you might not just read this book, you might very well DEVOUR it.  Not only is it as readable as a novel can be, but it is worthy of some serious anti-utopian reflection along the way.  And if you need any more convincing, ask the 96 percent of readers who have given it a four- or five-star rating (and of those, the fives are overwhelming).<br /><br />\\"Wool: Omnibus,\\" a collection of his first five \\"Wool\\" short stories of varying lengths, is a book that may well serve as a model for a new kind of fiction selling: self-publication on Kindle.  It is a trend that is creating of some serious consideration, and may well produce other great works along the way.<br /><br />As the self-publication story goes, Howey produced his first work and distributed it free of charge.  When it flew off the digital shelves he bowed to public pressure and published \\"Wool 2,\\" then on and on until this collection brought all five of the original short (to long) stories together.  At this time of writing, \\"Wool\\" is selling on the Kindle for $5.99, a terrific bargain.  It's worth it at triple the price or more.<br /><br />In a nutshell, Howey's tale of life in an underground silo is both convincing and well drawn, drawing its strength from both its compelling story and its ability to construct credible characters who seem alive and propel the action.  What he is also able to do is convince his readers that he has lived the life--not necessarily that of silo dwelling, but of working with nuts and bolts of machinery and computers and pumps and whatnot.  When he describes the guts of an engine he seems to know it down to the grease and gearing; when he speaks of being starved of oxygen and on the brink of drowning it seems so real one has to wonder if the author somehow writes from personal experience.  (The author was captain of a yacht...more on that later.)<br /><br />Given the nature of \\"Wool's\\" birth, I thought it might be interesting to look at the collection individually.  There will be NO PLOT SPOILERS.<br /><br />Wool 1 (or simply \\"Wool\\" when it was originally published) is the tale of a doomed man-- its sheriff, in fact--who has run afoul of the very society he had sworn to serve.  As the book's cover copy tells you, question the society and you are \\"granted\\" what its unwavering law demands: to leave.  To do so, one must take part in a \\"cleaning\\" of the outside cameras, a task that means certain death in the hostile outdoors.  Though we do not know why this is, the environment outside the silo is almost immediately toxic, even when wearing the bulky full-bodied and helmeted suit given to those making the exodus.  As dramatic as this story was, I actually liked it least of the five, as it was the only portion whose characterization took too long to develop and ultimately didn't really matter, as the characters were leaving us.  It was still interesting, but I felt much more invested as the story continued.<br /><br />I was drawn in more by the second chapter, \\"Proper Gauge.\\"  Here the silo's new mayor is tasked with looking for a new sheriff, and what I liked best about this portion of the story is the immersion of the characters and the readers into the literal and figurative depth of the tale, as they travel down to level 140: a journey of over a day (think of the World Trade Center shoved underground).  And what a mysterious and strange place this truly is.<br /><br />Here is where the story really showed its stuff, taking us down into the beast, letting us understand better how this society runs, how the various levels are lumped into cliques with their own identities, and that this frighteningly claustrophobic place has been running not just for decades, but for so many lifetimes that its duration is really unknown.  Lastly, in this tale the beginnings of a controversy form--one that will have repercussions through the rest of the novel.<br /><br />The third tale is \\"Casting Off,\\" as a newsheriff is found and transported to her new headquarters.  Here so much happens, and personalities become very important.  I think this portion did a terrific job of furthering not just the essential plot elements, but also of detailing how the personalities of the characters are becoming.  And, very importantly, the monolithic authority of those in charge begins to show its frightening face: not just because of the laws, but those at the hub of the society who enforce them.  And if you think that means the sheriff...you're dead wrong.<br /><br />\\"The Unraveling\\" is the the part of the story that jumps this tale into overdrive and becomes an undeniable page turner.  Author Howey really shows his skill here at dividing the story into sections and alternating those sections masterfully, giving us just enough to whet our appetites and keep us pushing forward to hope for resolutions to some extremely forceful conflicts.  As I am being mindful of spoilers I will only say that the author does indeed accomplish a feat, in that he invests you in both sides of the coin, letting the readers see WHY each side believes he or she is acting in the interest of their society, however misguided their opponent views him.  This is what I found most frightening, really: that people who feel THEY know best do the things they do...supposedly, on our behalf.<br /><br />And finally there is \\"The Stranded.\\"  The longest one of the five by far, it needs every page to accomplish its feats.  Howey threads the interweaving story lines together as well as any thriller author out there.  At the same time, however, he is able to invest us in the characters at the heart of the conflicts, again displaying their motivations and their earnest beliefs that they were doing their best for those they represented.  This, we find out, is exactly how this whole thing began hundreds of years ago: because people at the heart of things thought they were SAVING us.  It's that which I find horrifying...that and the fact that much of society just mills about oblivious, like those who brought lawn chairs to view the opening battles of the Civil War.  Or, more properly, perhaps: they never even realized that a battle was taking place, and cared little even if they did.  They only want to go about their business and be left alone.  I'm talking to YOU, there, Mr. and Mrs. Undecideds...<br /><br />But as to the pace and cadence of the story, I was amazed: as I travelled closer and closer to the end I found myself becoming more and more incapable of seeing how the author was going to end this thing with any promise of fulfillment on the reader's part.  But he does just that.<br /><br />And, again, I really feel Howey did a great job of making it feel that the author knew whereof he spoke: his technical scenes create such a sense of verisimilitude that they seem entirely realistic.  While it probably has much to do with Howey's own experience as a skipper of a yacht -an occupation that probably gave him a certain amount of freedom to write as well--his former occupation created for me a certain interesting contradiction.  On the open seas, with the vast, teeming, wide ocean in front of him, how did he conceive of such a confined, teeming, claustrophobic world?  He so perfectly imagined a society that ran vertically down into the earth while the wide sea ran across the horizon in front of him--I think that is an accomplishment unto itself.<br /><br />I can't help but wonder Howey's success through Kindle is indicative of the future of writing--and I do actually hope it will be.  What is important here is this, however: this is a great book, and you should buy it.  I won't be so audacious as to predict this will be in schoolrooms in the near future, taught alongside, say, [[ASIN:0451521234 1984]], but it tells a cautionary tale that is worthy of teaching our young.  As long as we have politicians who believe it is right to forgo pleasing the 47 % who disagree with them...that thosewho are \\"unlike\\" them are somehow worth less than those who are \\"like\\" them, we will run the risk of a future not unlike this one.  We have already give society the means of accomplishing global extermination--we run the risk every single day of encountering the next person (or oligarchy) who could put it into being.the fact that much of society just mills about oblivious, like those who brought lawn chairs to view the opening battles of the Civil War. For example, if I'm typing \\"review\\" and got as far as \\"revi\\" and a text comes in while I'm typing, it will turn it into \\"revirevirevi\\" and insert it into whatever I was typing. 0547884591|4.0|I gave Tim's new book four stars because while I immensely enjoyed the unconventional, quickest routes from A to B in cooking as well as learning and surviving on a mad planet, I found myself craving more detail on learning hacks for improving one's life  in general as well as for producing awesome food with minimal effort.<br /><br />Best part about the recipes? He claims only four ingredients per dish for simplicity of execution--bravo--who among us hasn't given up on 20 ingredient dishes that end up costing thrice what the resulting entree might cost in a good restaurant? Other &#34;insider&#34; tips from working chefs are as useful and satisfying as learning to pick locks with an inexpensive toolkit you can keepin your pocket, another life hack in Tim's smorgasbord of a book, under the Wild section on surviving. Teaching us the method of breaking chickens necks before preparing them or catching and killing city pigeons to survive is as disgusting as it is practical and I question their inclusion save for the worth of re-connecting us with how it's been done for 1000s of years and reminding us how removed we are from the coarse but necessary aspects of staying alive.<br /><br />I bought the book hoping to glean some useful nuggets of applied cleverness and high efficiency methodology for both cooking and streamlining my life. There's a lot of this in there, in both very entertaining and usable kind, but you'll have to consign yourself to jumping around a lot, something Tim encourages, as the book is ostensibly organized into relevant sections, but seems to have a lot off odds and ends strewn through. I picked us a &#34;like new&#34; used copy for $18 bucks and was surprised to get 500 pages and almost five pounds of product in my mailbox. Thus, I have a great coffee table book that I'll likely pick up for the short, sweet and to-the-point recipes on a regular basis. Learning to make ice cream in 30 seconds with liquid nitrogen? Great dinner party pyrotechnics. Using a bit of baking soda in the pot to make soft boiled eggs peel easier? Nice to know, but more likely an Internet &#34;how to&#34; than a must have in a hardcover tome purporting to revolutionize one's life.<br /><br />In conclusion, lots of good substance, but also an equal portion of dazzle dazzle, which while making for a fun read leaves me wanting more in hard-to-find insider info and hacks that might make one the prized life of the party as chief magician/chef/mind-reader/Bon vivant. I think this is Tim's aspiration, which he enthusiastically shares with us, but damn Tim...give me more of the &#34;how to make a million while seducing a princess over pomegranate crime br√ªl√©e&#34; info. I'd pay extra for that!

B007W8S2MG|5.0|I bought the PS2 version of this when it first came out, and I heard about enough new features in Golden that I bought a Vita just for this game. I've only gotten slightly past the first dungeon as of this review, but that was enough to see the biggest changes Golden made:<br />-Fusions have been made much, much easier. You can now pick what skills are passed down, instead of having to endlessly reroll important fusions. You can also search your possible fusions for the result persona; handy if you have something in mind but haven't memorized the fusion chart.<br />-Two new social links: Aeon and Jester. I've only seen Aeon so far, but it's a good link that's already made me laugh a few times.<br />-There's a \\"voice\\" feature which allows you to see what actions people have taken on each day of the game. For example, you can see which social links people hung out with the most, what days most people went to the dungeon, and so on. This feature also applies to fusion, so you can see what the most popular personas are at certain points.<br />-You can send out an SOS in the dungeon. People can respond to it to give you an HP and MP boost when you start your next battle, and you can respond to other people's SOSes to do the same for them. I've usually only seen a small boost since it's difficult to get more than one or two people to respond at a time, but every little bit helps. There's also a rescue log that lets you see the custom messages people made for their SOSes(taken from wordlists that include such fan-favorite phrases as \\"Meat Dimension\\" and \\"Bearsona\\").<br />-Some enemies have been tweaked. A big example of this is the boss of the first dungeon, but I won't spoil exactly how they changed it.<br />-Overall difficulty seems slightly harder than the original. There are also a wider range of difficulty levels now, ranging from Very Easy to Very Hard. I've heard that you can customize your own difficulty level on a New Game+ as well.<br />-Shuffle Time has been changed so that you select from any of the cards that are dealt. Certain cards will let you draw again, and there are bonuses if you manage to get every card in the hand.<br />-Skill cards can be gained from shuffle time & by leveling up certain personas, and you can register them and buy as many copies as you want.<br />-You can go out into town on most nights, and eat at Aiya, trade gems for items at the new Shiroku Pub, or hang out with a few social links.<br />-There's a new skip feature accessed by pressing start during dialog; this fast-forwards through scenes even faster than holding triangle does! Just be aware that some scenes from the original have new dialog in them, so keep a sharp eye out for that if you're skipping through them.<br />-Your characters can wear outfits to change their appearance in the dungeon now. You start out with three outfits, and gain more through various methods.<br /><br />So old players of Persona 4 can see what's in it for them. As for newcomers to the series, Persona 4 was already a solid RPG before these changes. It has a great story, an excellent battle system that keeps things fast-paced and tense, and a good balance between dungeon crawling and the rest of the game. It also has great replay value, since there's some bonus content unlocked by playing through a second time, your character's mental stats(Understanding, Diligance, Expression, Knowledge, and Courage) carry over to make it much easier to complete all the social links, and you get the chance to see the other character in social links that give you a choice between two people's stories.

0525952926|2.0|I have read several of Ken Follett's books, and particularly enjoyed the historical perspective and well-researched accounts he gave in World Without End and Pillars. This series, however, seems like a watered-down, pop version of history. Unfortunately, Follett seems to have gotten to that stage in his career where his work no longer needs to be an impactful, moving, and memorable work of literature. Instead, his name will bring enough people to buy the book just based off the nostalgia they feels for his previous, much greater works.<br /><br />The major flaw I saw in this work was the very obvious political agenda Follett pushed. The diversity and attention to minority and progressive issues was forced into each character relationship, and it seemed artificial and tedious rather than natural. Although I appreciate reading about the issues that faced marginalized groups during that period, he overused and exhausted these issues to far beyond the point of historical insight and humanitarian concern. Reading this book felt like taking a series of courses in African American history, civil rights, women's rights, handicapped rights, the history of antisemitism, gay lesbian and bisexual rights, and racial prejudices. When he did mention conservative politics or Christianity, it often was accompanied by negative undertones. Regardless of one's politics, I expected the same standard of accurate presentation of the complicated nature of history, rather than this very simplified, almost childish black-and-white, good-and-evil story.<br /><br />As being born in the Soviet Union myself, my family has personally experienced the treachery and the unexpected humanity from both the Nazi and Stalinist regimes. Follett had a great opportunity to present another, hidden side of history, and the story of the men and women caught up in Fascism who fervently believed they were doing good. Instead, it seems every Nazi soldier he depicts is the same stereotypical impulsive sadist who,as if it wasn't enough to make him personally barbaric, was also portrayed as physically disgusting. If history has taught us anything, it is that nothing is ever so clearly black and white, and all aggressors were seen as hero's by some society or another. This book is a colossal failure in giving people a different perspective on the causes of evils that took place in WW2. Instead, Follett has made this book an easy 3-day read with the simplistic dichotomy between good and evil equivalent to that of a children's novel.

Although the plot is relatively simple, the storytelling by legal literary legend Grisham on how Malcolm Bannister gets his revenge is exquisitely complex.  This is an intricate legal mystery that requires the reader to pay close attention as there is a lot going on.  Fortunately, this will not be a problem as Mr. Grisham's engrossing writing style will keep the reader involved.<br />The Racketeer centers on the gruesome murder mystery of a Federal Judge.  Disbarred attorney Malcolm Bannister is serving a 10 year Federal prison sentence for supposed RICO (Racketeer Influence and Corrupt Organization Act) money laundering that he was innocent of.  While in prison, he discovers who killed the Federal judge.  Bargaining this explosive information for early sentence commutation, A fearful Bannister requests and enters the Witness Protection Program and the story takes off in earnest.  I hesitate to say more as I do not want to give away any spoilers.  Needless to say, not all appears to be what it is in this well crafted novel as Mr. Grisham takes the reader on a wild legal mystery ride.<br />Character development was absolutely superb. B00917IQFY|5.0|\\"Three Chords Good\\", the reunion album by Graham Parker and the Rumour, is not a throwback to the classic Graham Parker & The Rumour albums of the late 1970s thru 1980.  Anyone looking for a redo of that explosiveness will probably be disappointed.  But they shouldn't be.<br /><br />While \\"Three Chords Good\\" is less aggressive than those early GP & Rumour albums, it is arguably still just as good.  They are not trying to sound like they are still in their 20s and early 30s.  The sound captured on \\"Three Chords Good\\" is of a band in their 60s.  That means it's a more reflective and less juiced up sound.  But the songs are still extraordinary and the album rocks as much as it needs to.  The playing of The Rumour is more subtle than on the early GP albums, but still top notch.  These are exceptionally talented musicians and it shows.<br /><br />The Rumour were one of the great backing bands and what a shame that Graham Parker chose to disengage from them after 1980.  While that didn't stop Graham Parker from releasing many great albums in the 3 decades since, it was still a tragic mistake to disassociate from The Rumour.  Since The Rumour were arguably one of the great backing bands ever, what came after from Graham Parker could only compare negatively since no matter how good the playing might have been and no matter how great the songs and strong the albums were, the end result could never be quite as brilliantly realized as would have been the case, both live and on record.  That's perceptive reality, whether it's justified or not.<br /><br />Regardless of what might have or could have been, the bottom line is that \\"Three Chords Good\\" is a classic Graham Parker and The Rumour album.  They could have chosen to package it with the same type of aggressive in-your-face playing and production as on \\"Squeezing Out Sparks\\".  I would not have complained had they done that, but that would have been the easy choice to make.  With \\"Three Chords Good\\", Graham Parker and The Rumour are not afraid to forgo recapturing old glories so as to chart out new ones.

B009L32R5I|3.0|If someone told you they intended to re-make the movie Blade Runner, you'd be like \\"what's the point? You'll never get more powerful performances or cinematography no matter how hard you try\\". That's why I'm so torn on this record. It's such a faithful tribute, that in many cases it is nearly an exact replica of prior work. And please understand I'm coming at this from the standpoint of being a huge Hackett fan (and accordingly that era of Genesis music).<br /><br />There's no doubt, the double-album playlist is a fantastic selection of songs, and Hackett's playing is simply immaculate. The variety of vocalists adds some interesting & unique flavors (Nad Sylvan sounds like a mutant cross between Gabriel, Collins, and Edward G Robinson). Some new intros or parts have been written here and there to freshen things up, and the sound quality is outstanding. This is an exceptionally well engineered record.<br /><br />But in a way, I wish Hackett had re-tumbled the arrangements, instrumentation, and vibe to create his own interpretation of the pieces. A lot of times it sounds like old Genesis with a guest singer. So much of Gabriel's appeal (and early Collins) was his unique vocal inflections and character. About half of this record has the vocalists adding their personality which I love, but the other half feels like an attempt to match everything. It's like trying to re-do Freddy Mercury; overall a futile effort, so might as well do your own take.<br /><br />It was clearly a monumental amount of work to put this together, and for $11 it's a lovely spin down memory lane. I still get chills listening to some of the songs which identify so strongly with a certain period of my life. But ultimately there's just not enough new content to set it apart from the old records. I get the sense that Hackett just wanted to play these songs again, as his original contributions still rank as brilliant. Respectfully, I'd say save it for the live shows and make records that truly \\"re-imagine\\".

0446310786|5.0|It's been a very, very long time since I read To Kill a Mockingbird - maybe since I was in school myself. But teaching it to my students gave me the chance to explore the novel all over again and to remind myself just how masterful it truly is. What almost everyone remembers about the novel is the trial at the heart of it, and that's for good reason; it's still infuriating, disheartening, and effective, even all these years after the book's publication. But there's so much more to the novel; after all, the whole first half of the book is more about life in Maycomb, from the perils of the first day of school to the difficulties of navigating the complex social strata that make up the town (from middle class to the working poor to the white trash, from the whites to the blacks, from the foot-washing Baptists to the more moderate Baptists elsewhere in town). More than that, though, To Kill a Mockingbird immerses you in the perspective of a young girl who is only just beginning to understand the world, and that choice - to let Scout tell the tale, instead of the rapidly maturing Jem or the wise and thoughtful Atticus - is what really makes To Kill a Mockingbird so remarkable. Without Scout's narration, the novel could easily become any other novel about a Southern town, and the trial just another book about racism as perceived by the white folks who were almost entirely unaffected by it. But in letting Scout tell the story, it becomes a way of exploring the complexities of the world through the eyes of someone who understands none of them - and even more importantly, accepts none of them. That dramatic irony between what we know and what Scout knows makes the book even richer to explore, as we begin to realize that so much of what we take for granted flies in the face of the way we raise our children and expect them to live. If that sounds heavy-handed and moralizing, well, that's me, and not the book; in fact, To Kill a Mockingbird soars by telling its story without ever resorting to preaching, hectoring, or belaboring its points. Instead, it simply shows us a young girl who is forced to look at the uglier side of human nature, only to see that there may be more good in people than we think. It's a book that's rich in humanity, life, characterization, language, and so much more, and it's a tragedy that Harper Lee never wrote anything else in her life. But what could compare to such a perfect gem as this?er resorting to preaching, hectoring, or belaboring its points. Instead, it simply shows us a young girl who is forced to look at the uglier side of human nature, only to see that there may be more good in people than we think. It's a book that's rich in humanity, life, characterization, language, and so much more, and it's a tragedy that Harper Lee never wrote anything else in her life. But what could compare to such a perfect gem as this?

B008ELUEQC|4.0|\\"Roderick David Stewart\\"--best known as just good ol' Rod 'The Bod' Stewart--has released the very first holiday album of his career, entitled [[ASIN:B008ELUEQC Merry Christmas, Baby]], and it's by far one of the best holiday albums I've heard in years. I suppose that's to be expected when your album producer is the inimitable David Foster, who also plays piano and keyboards on nearly all 13 tracks. The production is spot-on, being laid-back with minimal instrumentation when needed, and full-on big-band and strings on uptempo songs. In the U.S., his first single is a duet with Mary J. Blige, the chillingly gorgeous and beautiful version of \\"We Three Kings (of Orient Are).\\" I have never, ever heard a more beautiful version of this classic carol. In the UK, he's released the opening track, an equally beautiful, lush and rich take on the WWII-era \\"Have Yourself a Merry Little Christmas.\\" One of the featured musicians is probably the best session guitarist in the U.S. (and has been for decades), Dean Parks, who is regularly highlighted here, and so is Troy \\"Trombone Shorty\\" Andrews, who receives named credit on nearly every track on which he plays. Another triumph is his \\"duet\\" with the First Lady of Jazz, the late Ella Fitzgerald, on the great ballad \\"What Are You Doing New Year's Eve?\\" Using the recording technique first introduced in 1991 by Natalie Cole when she \\"duetted\\" with her late father, Nat \\"King\\" Cole, at the time considered revolutionary, on the critically-acclaimed [[ASIN:B001CVT0GG Unforgettable: With Love]], Stewart's and Fitzgerald's voices blend in such a way that you'd think that they had been singing together for decades.<br /><br />And you can't help but smile and tap your feet in the groove on the title track, \\"Merry Christmas, Baby.\\" It's another duet, with Cee Lo Green, who seems to be everywhere lately. He's just now finishing up his contract in the live rounds of the fall 2012 cycle of the wildly popular hit NBC series \\"The Voice\\" (he'll be replaced by the young and smooth crooner Usher Raymond when the next cycle begins in winter 2013). Stewart and Green sound like they're having a ball, and you can't help but have one, too. It's one of my very favorite tracks on the CD. (Being a huge fan of \\"The Voice,\\" I could just imagine the two of them recording it, because they recorded it together in a Los Angeles studio, where Green is based). \\"Red-Suited Super Man\\" is another uptempo, fun song, written by Stewart and both David Foster and his daughter Amy--a powerhouse of a pop-song composer in today's market.<br /><br />Although I'm also a big fan of Michael Buble, his duet with Rod of \\"Winter Wonderland\\" was no wonderland for me. Stewart is a high tenor and Buble is a baritone, so their ranges don't mix at all, and their attempts to compensate with harmony seem forced. It is, for me, the only failure on the album. His sweet takes on \\"Santa Claus Is Coming to Town,\\" \\"White Christmas\\" (singing the opening verse would have been nice, but yet it is so rarely done!), and \\"Let It Snow! Let It Snow! Let It Snow!\\" (featuring Dave Koz on tenor saxophone) were all lovely. Again, David Foster's arrangements, especially on strings, rendered these tracks exquisite.<br /><br />And rather than using the iconic Elvis Presley version of \\"Blue Christmas,\\" Foster elects to go further back in time to when it was a straight ballad with lush strings, and it works perfectly. I don't equate \\"When You Wish Upon a Star\\" as a holiday song, but Stewart delivers it effectively. The last two tracks close out the CD perfectly. \\"Silent Night\\" is tender and beautiful, as the Page Los Angeles Children's Chorus joins in to make it so lovely, and adult background singers then were added to make the track absolutely spectacular. But he saved--probably his personal favorite--for last. Ever the Scotsman, he delivered \\"Auld Lang Syne\\" as a co-arranger with Foster, singing all of the Scottish-to-English verses and arranged for a wee bit of synthesizer-bagpipes for flavor.<br /><br />This album brings to a close, at least for the meantime, Rod's hugely successful experimentation with big bands, strings, and as it is so well known, \\"The Great American Songbook.\\" His catalogue includes [[ASIN:B00006JL4J It Had to Be You... The Great American Songbook]]; [[ASIN:B0000C9ZEX As Time Goes By...The Great American Songbook: Volume II]]; [[ASIN:B0002X94Y8 Stardust... The Great American Songbook, Vol. III]]; [[ASIN:B000B7HZXM Thanks For The Memory...The Great American Songbook IV]]; and [[ASIN:B003X5F4R8 Fly Me To The Moon...The Great American Songbook Volume V]].<br /><br />This holiday album would seem to bring this style of singing for him to a close, for he is releasing, in March 2013, an all-new CD/mp3 album of rock songs he has written or co-written. For those of you who bought this album via retail, between a tag on the outer plastic covering and an insert within the CD, the new owners of this CD can download a total of four tracks of Stewart's songs, including the first track from his new rock album and three classic Rod Stewart tracks of yore.  This is quite a Christmas gift from Stewart to his fans.<br /><br />Why four stars and not five? Well, as of this review, he's a man of 67 and about to turn 68 in January 2013. His voice is beginning to sound as such, especially when he attemps to reach down in his lower register. It's always been gritty, but now it's hoarse and sometimes harsh to the listener. He's best when he reaches for his upper register and falsetto, but not every song affords him that luxury. It's clear that his singing voice, while quite good when the arrangement works for him, is definitely in decline. I couldn't help but think of Frank Sinatra's voice as he aged while listening to this album, although Rod's voice isn't anywhere near Sinatra's level of deterioration right now. And, as I said in the review, \\"Winter Wonderland\\" with Michael Buble was a total bust--and that absolutely floored me. I was SO expecting another great duet, but it was an enormous letdown.<br /><br />If I could, I would give the album, 4-1/2 stars, but I can't, so therefore...<br /><br />CD REVIEW:  **** (out of 5).....BOBBY BOURBEAU.....3 December 2012r-bagpipes for flavor.<br /><br />This album brings to a close, at least for the meantime, Rod's hugely successful experimentation with big bands, strings, and as it is so well known, \\"The Great American Songbook.\\" His catalogue includes [[ASIN:B00006JL4J It Had to Be You... 0385536828|4.0|Ian McEwan's Sweet Tooth is one of those deceiving novels in which, for page after page, nothing much seems to be happening until enough small pieces suddenly fit together to reveal another segment of the puzzle.  And, in the meantime, the main players are, bit by bit, turning into real flesh and blood people whose lives outside the pages of the books are easily imagined.  Serena Frome, who supplies the narrative voice of Sweet Tooth, is one such character.<br /><br />It is 1972 and Serena, bright as she is, knows that even her superior Cambridge education will not guarantee her a spot on a career path she deserves.  The corporate and academic worlds of 1972 Britain are, after all, still very much dominated by men, so her employment options are limited.  But during this Cold War period, British Intelligence sees Cambridge as an important source of new recruits to the service and, when she is \\"tapped on the shoulder\\" by a professor/lover who identifies candidates for MI5, Serena accepts the resulting job offer.<br /><br />She has no idea what to expect next, or even an inkling of how drastically this one decision will impact the rest of her life.  Now, after the passage of almost four decades, she begins her story in the book's opening paragraph this way:<br /><br /> \\"My name is Serena Frome (rhymes with plume) and almost forty years ago I was sent on a secret mission for the British security service.  I didn't return safely.  Within eighteen months of joining I was sacked, having disgraced myself and ruined my lover, though he certainly had a hand in his own undoing.\\"<br /><br />In the last sentences of the first chapter, speaking of her lover, Serena offers another hint of what is to follow:<br /><br /> \\"His case was more complex and sadder than anyone knew.  He would change my life and behave with selfless cruelty as he prepared to set out on a journey with no hope of return.  If I know so little about him even now, it's because I accompanied him only a very small part of the way.\\"<br /><br />Serena Frome, as it turns out, is as finely crafted a fictional character as one could hope to find.  She is certainly not easy to like, but she is easy to understand; she can be frustratingly boring one moment, and be taking stupid chances with her future the next; and she is as likely to let her innate intelligence be neutralized by her equally innate naivet&eacute; as the other way around.  She is, in other words, human.<br /><br />Above all else, however, readers will probably remember Sweet Tooth because of its ending, one likely either to infuriate them or to leave them smiling in admiration - one or the other.  Sweet Tooth is so much more than the sum of its parts that, as I expected from already being a fan of McEwan's previous novels, I find myself in the \\"smiling with admiration\\" camp.part of the way.\\"<br /><br />Serena Frome, as it turns out, is as finely crafted a fictional character as one could hope to find. B004EWFV9K|4.0|Mankell is one of my favorite Scandinavian authors and one of the most consistent.  He does climb onto the soapbox at times and he is on it a little in this story, but I can forgive him his idiosyncrasies because he writes a very real and moving story in this episode of Kurt Wallander's life.  Already a morose and brooding character, Wallander has now buried his father and is beginning to stare his own mortality in the face at the age of sixty.  Loneliness is beginning to set in and Wallander is visited by a past love that just adds to his despair.  The only positive news is that his first granddaughter, Klara, is born.  Overwhelming life issues including failing memory begin to take its toll on Wallander and when it seems that it can't get any worse, it does.<br /><br />The Troubled Man refers to both our protagonist, Kurt, as he tracks down a potential Swedish spy following the disappearance of his daughters' significant other's parents and the to the newly introduced paternal grandfather of Klara as he runs from something in his past.  The story has legs and gets its basis from the Cold War when Sweden cast its eyes East in fear and West in admiration.  The country was split on whether to join NATO and the general populace had its collective hands full of political intrigue.  Past events are woven into the fabric of this mystery and although the plot is well played out, it takes a back seat to the life and times of our main protagonist in this long series.  The story requires this reviewer to stop here so that other pieces of the stories can be left to the reader to discover.<br /><br />While the story is probably too long, and too deeply troubled and the mystery is not perfect, I will forgive Mankell as a greater and more important character study was in progress requiring his strong writing skills and mental toughness to complete. Will they migrate and establish a fishery?<br /><br />It turns out that, in the real world, farmed salmon do run.  A bit later as they first return to where they escaped or were released, then they miss the anglers who show up earlier to get the native fish, and then they screw up the spawning grounds by trying to lay their own eggs, so there's lots of emerging reasons not to farm salmon as we currently do, but that's not this film.  The point is, the fish DO run, they swim upstream and over the damn (and they swam and they swam right over the dam!).<br /><br />But this is a Western film, with Brits abroad, and it's set in Yemen and all we know about the Middle East is that there's a whole lot of terrorists over there, so that's the way the movie presents the Yemenis.  If they're not just standing around providing local color with their antiquated dress and weather sculpted faces, they're ordering assassinations, and trying to destroy things.  Don't ask why.  Don't consider whether it's our policies that might be causing this animosity, our treatment of the Arab on the street.  That isn't this movie, either.  Worse, the two British characters wander around in casual western wear, no more than one layer, nothing covering their arms or her head, just as if Yemen is a branch of Hyde park.  When she can't sleep, she comes out of  her tent in her pjs, then he comes out in his, and they sit next to each other.  No matter how this might outrage the locals in the real world, or get the Brits killed (it doesn't, the plot won't allow it).<br /><br />The movie is a rather simple romantic adventure, a boy and a girl join a rich sheik in his dream to bring salmon fishing to Yemen.  A few cranky old Arab terrorists and some cynical British politicians get in the way, but in the end &#34;Faith&#34; and Love triumph, and the salmon run.<br /><br />A lot of people thought this was an incredible film.

B009DQQF7Y|5.0|This is a great new album from GY!BE, as if it were only last year that their last one, YANQUI U.X.O., was released (it was actually late 2002, almost exactly 10 years before 'ALLELUJAH!).  The material was mostly written back in the 2002-2003 timeframe, so it's not surprising that there is strong continuity.  Like the 2002 album, the new one is entirely instrumental, without the prominent vocal samples that were an integral part of the sound of their earlier recordings.  There are two main tracks, and two shorter \\"drones\\" without the intensity and cinematic sweep of the longer pieces:<br /><br />1) Mladic<br />2) Their Helicopters Sing (drone)<br />3) We Drift Like Worried Fire<br />4) Strung Like Lights at Thee Printemps Erable (drone)<br /><br />'ALLELUJAH hits harder with more tribal percussion than the last (though the idea that YANQUI U.X.O. was a quiet album is quite absurd) and the Steve Reich influence is less noticeable.  Sophie Trudeau's electric violin is one of the most distinctive elements of the band's sound, over massed guitars and percussion.<br /><br />The lineup hasn't changed too much.  The 8-piece band has only a few subtractions and additions:<br /><br />Thierry Amar -- electric bass, contrabass, cello<br />David Bryant -- electric guitar, dulcimer, portasound, kemence<br />Bruce Cawdron -- drums, vibraphone, marimba, glockenspiel<br />Aidan Girt -- drums<br />Efrim Menuck -- electric guitar, hurdy gurdy<br />Michael Moya -- electric guitar<br />Mauro Pezzente -- bass guitar<br />Sophie Trudeau -- violin, SK5<br />(and Karl Lemieux -- 16mm film projections)<br /><br />Another reviewer says the album was recorded by the four core members -- that is not correct, it is a misreading of the liner notes.  Two of the tracks were mixed by four of the band members, but the entire band plays throughout.  This is a leftist collective after all, no elitism!<br /><br />I had the good fortune to finally see GY!BE on tour playing the album on October 5th in Atlanta.  It was a great performance, with no vocals and no light on stage drawing attention to any of the individual musicians.  A primitive looking collage of videos and still photos is projected throughout the concert.  Without any lyrics,  powerful moods of despair, anger, defiance, hope, and ecstasy are conveyed, and a utopian vision of an egalitarian world without war, poverty, or ecological destruction.<br /><br />The images that accompanied the high point of the night, \\"We Drift Like Worried Fire,\\" were fires burning, the burning of fossil fuels that is destroying the climate and the ecosystem, it seemed clear to me.  With Canada now madly mining the tar sands of Alberta and the U.S. fracking up the land, we are clearly moving in the wrong direction.<br /><br />I am glad that GY!BE decided to reunite -- their vision is an inspiration to keep up the fight for the future. A lot more information on the aliens (in this show, the thrumming noise they make actually gives me the creeps, and monsters usually don't scare me), more information about the children taken by them and what happens to them, much more edge of your seat action. So, it often pays to stick with a show, and that includes TNT, who gets a high five from me for not dropping this one, something NBC and ABC do way to often to the point that I don't even watch new shows on those networks. Why get hooked when it's just going to get canceled? Nevertheless, you need to buy and watch Season 1 to understand the characters and stories in Season 2. So definitely start from the beginning and understand that Season 2 will knock your socks off! The only thing I am upset about now is that we have to wait until next summer for Season 3!s. B004LWZWGK|5.0|It has been eight years since the Batman took the wrap for the murder of District Attorney Harvey Dent. Eight years since the last time the Dark Knight was spotted in Gotham. The streets are safe, the police are receiving praise for doing a good job--except Commissioner James Gordon knows it's all based on a lie. About to come clean of what really happened that fateful night, Gotham is suddenly thrown into chaos at the hands of a mastermind, muscle-loaded criminal named Bane. With the city about to fall, the Batman must return to restore order to his beloved city otherwise it will fall into the hands of a sadistic genius bent on its destruction. To complicate matters, a mysterious female cat burglar is working out an agenda of her own and her endgame is tied into the legacy of Bruce Wayne.<br /><br />Will Batman rise from the shadows to defeat evil once more, or has he had his day and should have stayed in the dark?<br /><br />Saw the midnight screening of this gem before it hit theatres all over the world. This movie is epic on a scale that is hard to fit into a simple review, especially since I don't want to give away any key plot points and/or spoilers.<br /><br />The Dark Knight Rises picks up immediately after The Dark Knight storyline-wise, and eight years later in movie-time. Running throughout the whole flick are threads from Batman Begins and Dark Knight, plotlines that reach their ultimate climax in what I have to say is one of the best endings to a trilogy I've ever seen. It's on par with, third-movie-wise, Return of the Jedi and Return of the King. All comes to a head as we're led down a deep tunnel into who Bruce Wayne (Christian Bale) truly is and what being Batman has done to him. Glimpses of his scarred psyche were hinted at in the previous two movies, but really get hit home in an emotional and powerful way throughout this final installment.<br /><br />Batman himself also shines as he gets to show off his physical skill against a villain that can truly stand toe-to-toe with him, something we never saw in the previous two films. The battle with Bane (Tom Hardy) is realistic, strongly-delivered, and one where this reviewer felt the punches thrown as if it was happening to him. Yeah, it was that good of a fight.<br /><br />The other Bat-flicks struggled with having two villains in the same movie. To be honest, I never thought I'd see the day where a superhero movie would have more than one villain and be just as good of a movie as if it had just one. Anne Hathaway's Catwoman is the best rendition of the character I've seen on screen, both in movies and on TV. She had to play multiple roles given her identity as a thief and work her deception in such a way that a lot of the time we weren't sure who's side she was on. I'm an Anne Hathaway fan, but this movie easily contains her best career performance to date.<br /><br />Bane was a crazy good villain, a kind of cross between Joker--intelligence-wise--and Ra's Al Ghul--combat-wise--of the previous two movies. Especially since most of his face was covered with a mask throughout the whole flick, Tom Hardy had to act with his eyes in such a way as to deliver a performance as if he wasn't wearing a mask at all. It was something he did in spades. Bane was one of those on-screen villains that you were afraid of because he's that smart and that powerful and that sadistic.<br /><br />Gary Oldman did an amazing job, as usual, as Jim Gordon, and Sir Michael Caine nailed it once again as Alfred. In fact, I'd be shocked if Sir Michael didn't get an Oscar nomination for his emotional portrayal of a hard-headed vigilante's butler.<br /><br />It'd be so easy to give away several key plot points in this review, but I'm keeping it vague on purpose because you simply need to see this movie for yourself. You might think you have it figured out, but you'd be wrong, my friend.<br /><br />All dangling story threads from the previous two movies are resolved, the SFX did its job but the movie didn't rely on it, and The Dark Knight Rises had one of the best movie endings in history, to me, one equal to the incredibly-satisfying ending of The Shawshank Redemption.<br /><br />Hats off to director Christopher Nolan and crew for the amazing stories and respect they delivered to Bat-fans everywhere throughout the entire Dark Knight Trilogy.<br /><br />Good watch Batman.
